Yes, I can help you with that problem!

For this problem, we can use the multinomial theorem, which states that the expansion of $(x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_k)^n$ has ${n+k-1 \choose k-1}$ terms, where the expansion of each term is of the form $x_1^{a_1}x_2^{a_2}\cdots x_k^{a_k}$, where $a_1, a_2, \ldots, a_k$ are non-negative integers that satisfy $a_1 + a_2 + \cdots + a_k = n$.

So for our problem of finding the coefficient of $x^3y^3z^2$ in the expansion of $(x+y+z)^8$, we need to find the number of terms in the expansion of $(x+y+z)^8$ that have $x^3$, $y^3$, and $z^2$ as their exponents. We can set up the equation $3a_1 + 3a_2 + 2a_3 = 8$, where $a_1$ represents the exponent of $x$, $a_2$ represents the exponent of $y$, and $a_3$ represents the exponent of $z$.

Simplifying the equation, we get $a_1 + a_2 + \frac{2}{3}a_3 = \frac{8}{3}$. We can see that $a_1$ and $a_2$ must be integers, since they represent the exponents of $x$ and $y$. Therefore, we can just try out the possible values of $a_1$ and $a_2$ that satisfy the equation above and find the corresponding value of $a_3$ that also satisfies the equation.

The possible values of $a_1$ are 0, 1, 2, and 3, and the possible values of $a_2$ are also 0, 1, 2, and 3. When $a_1 = 0$, we have $a_2 = 2$ and $a_3 = 2$. When $a_1 = 1$, we have $a_2 = 1$ and $a_3 = 3$. When $a_1 = 2$, we have $a_2 = 0$ and $a_3 = 4/3$. When $a_1 = 3$, we have $a_2 = -1$, which is not valid.

Therefore, there are two terms in the expansion of $(x+y+z)^8$ that have $x^3y^3z^2$ as their exponents. Using the multinomial theorem, the coefficient of each of these terms is ${8+3-1 \choose 3-1}{6+3-1 \choose 3-1}{1+2-1 \choose 2-1} = 105 \cdot 28 \cdot 1 = 2940$.

So the coefficient of $x^3y^3z^2$ in the expansion of $(x+y+z)^8$ is $2 \cdot 2940 = \boxed{5880}$.

If $a \geq 4$, then $a^3+b^2+c>a^3\geq 4^3>50$. But we want $a^3+b^2+c \leq 50$, so we must have $a=2$.  Now we substitute $a=2$ into $a^3+b^2+c \leq 50$, which gives $b^2+c\leq 42$. Since $b^2<42$, we know that $b$ must be one of 2, 4, or 6.

When $b=2,$ $c\leq 38$. There are 19 even positive integers less than or equal to 38, namely $2\times 1$, $2\times 2$, $\ldots$, $2\times 19$.

When $b=4,$ $c\leq 26$. There are 13 even positive integers less than or equal to 26.

When $b=6,$ $c\leq 6$. There are 3 even positive integers less than or equal to 6.

Thus the answer is $19+13+3=\boxed{35}$.

Of course! Here's one way to approach the problem:

Because $a$ is even, we can write $a = 2n$ for some positive integer $n$. Then $a^3 = (2n)^3 = 8n^3$, so the inequality becomes:

$$8n^3 + b^2 + c \leq 50$$

We can simplify this by subtracting $b^2$ and $c$ from both sides:

$$8n^3 \leq 50 - b^2 - c$$

Now we want to count the number of ways to choose even positive integers $n$, $b$, and $c$ that satisfy this inequality. We can do this by checking all possible values of $b$ and $c$ and counting the number of $n$ that work.

First, notice that $c$ must be less than or equal to $50$ in order for the inequality to hold. If $c > 50$, then we could decrease $c$ by some amount and increase $n$ by a larger amount, without affecting the left side of the inequality. So let's consider all possible values of $b$ and $c$ such that $b^2 + c \leq 50$.

For each such value of $b$ and $c$, we can compute the largest possible value of $n$ that satisfies the inequality by solving:

$$8n^3 = 50 - b^2 - c$$

Since $8n^3$ is an increasing function of $n$, this equation has at most one solution for $n$. If it has a solution, then we know that all even positive integers greater than or equal to $n$ also satisfy the inequality, since they have larger cubes.

So here's the plan: we'll loop through all possible values of $b$ and $c$ such that $b^2 + c \leq 50$, and for each pair $(b,c)$ we'll solve the equation $8n^3 = 50 - b^2 - c$. If this equation has a solution, we'll count all even positive integers greater than or equal to $n$ as valid solutions of $(a,b,c)$, and sum up those counts to get the total number of valid triples.

Here's some Python code that implements this plan:

```
count = 0
for b in range(1,8):  # b^2 <= 50, so b <= 7
    for c in range(1,43):  # c <= 50 - b^2, so c <= 42
        if b**2 + c <= 50:
            n3 = (50 - b**2 - c) // 8
            if n3 >= 1:
                count += (n3 + 1) // 2  # count even integers >= n
print(count)
```

The probability that the second number is more than $\frac14$ unit greater than the first number decreases linearly from $\frac34$ to $0$ as the first number increases linearly from $0$ to $\frac34$. Sure, Id be happy to explain this problem to you! It sounds like youre working on probability problems in your precalculus class. 

For this problem, were asked to find the probability that the second number chosen will be greater than the first number by more than $\frac 14$ unit. Lets break this down into smaller steps.

First, let the first number chosen be $x$. Then, we need to choose a second number, $y$, such that $y>x+\frac 14$. This means that the distance between $y$ and $x$ must be greater than $\frac 14$ unit.

Lets draw a number line to help visualize this. Let $x$ be some point on the number line between 0 and 1. We want to find the probability that the next number we choose, $y$, falls somewhere to the right of $x+\frac 14$ on the number line.

[asy]
size(8cm);
draw((0,0)--(3,0),Arrow);
label("$0$",(0,0),SW);
label("$1$",(3,0),SE);
real x = 0.7; // example value of x
draw((x,-0.2)--(x,0.2));
label("$x$",(x,-0.2),S);
draw((x+0.25,-0.2)--(x+0.25,0.2));
label("$x+\frac{1}{4}$",(x+0.25,-0.2),S);
[/asy]

To find the probability that $y>x+\frac 14$, we can find the length of the segment on the number line that satisfies this condition, and divide by the total length of the number line between 0 and 1.

The length of the segment where $y$ is greater than $x+\frac 14$ is $1-(x+\frac 14)$, since any number $y$ greater than $x+\frac 14$ must be to the right of $x+\frac 14$, and hence between $x+\frac 14$ and 1 (inclusive). 

The total length of the number line between 0 and 1 is 1. Therefore, the probability that $y>x+\frac 14$ is $\frac{1-(x+\frac 14)}{1} = \boxed{\frac{3}{4}-x}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
First, let's find all distinct pairs of points in the set $S$. There are $3^2 = 9$ possible pairs.

Next, consider the number of distinct lines passing through each of these pairs of points.

- If both points have the same $x$-coordinate, then the line passing through them is vertical and there is only one such line for each pair.
- If both points have the same $y$-coordinate, then the line passing through them is horizontal and there is only one such line for each pair.
- If the points do not have the same $x$- or $y$-coordinate, then the line passing through them has a slope of $\pm 1$ and there are two such lines for each pair (one with slope $1$, the other with slope $-1$).

Therefore, for each pair of distinct points in $S$, there are either one or two distinct lines passing through them. To count the total number of distinct lines passing through at least two members of $S$, we can count the number of pairs of points with one line and the number of pairs with two lines, and add them up.

- For pairs of points with one line, there are $6$ pairs of points with a vertical line passing through them, $6$ pairs of points with a horizontal line passing through them, and no pairs of points with a line of slope $\pm 1$. Therefore, there are $6+6=12$ pairs of points with one line passing through them.
- For pairs of points with two lines, there are $4$ pairs of points with two lines of slope $1$ passing through them, $4$ pairs of points with two lines of slope $-1$ passing through them, and $1$ pair of points with a line of slope $1$ and a line of slope $-1$ passing through them. Therefore, there are $4+4+1=9$ pairs of points with two lines passing through them.

Adding the number of pairs of points with one line ($12$) to the number of pairs with two lines ($9$), we get a total of $\boxed{21}$ distinct lines passing through at least two members of $S$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's break down the problem into smaller parts. We can count the number of natural numbers that have two 6s, three 6s, and so on, then sum up these counts to get the final answer.

Number of natural numbers with two 6s:

We have to choose two positions for the 6s, and the remaining digit can be any of the eight non-6 digits. There are ${3 \choose 2}$ ways to choose the positions for the 6s, and $8$ choices for the remaining digit. So the total count is ${3 \choose 2} \cdot 8 = 24.$

Number of natural numbers with three 6s:

We have to choose three positions for the 6s, and the remaining digit can be any of the eight non-6 digits. There are ${3 \choose 3}$ ways to choose the positions for the 6s, and $8$ choices for the remaining digit. So the total count is ${3 \choose 3} \cdot 8 = 8.$

Note that we cannot have natural numbers with four or more 6s, because the largest such number is 6666, which is greater than 800.

Thus the total count is $24 + 8 = \boxed{32}.$
